### 63. **Simos:**

this is my final take on vitamin d into chapter 

Chapter 3: Vitamin D 

3.1 Introduction 
Vitamin D, often called the "sunshine vitamin", is synthesized in the skin through exposure to ultraviolet B (UVB, wavelength 290 - 315 nm) radiation from the sun. After UVB photons penetrate the skin and activate 7-dehydrocholesterol to previtamin D3 (photoconversion), which then isomerizes to vitamin D3 (cholecalciferol) (Holick, 2007). Vitamin D is the driver of a sophisticated system, called the “vitamin D endocrine system (VDES)”, and undergoes a two-step enzymatic activation (Norman, 2008). 
First, in the liver (cytochrome P450) it is hydroxylated by the enzyme 25-hydroxylase (CYP2R1) to form 25-hydroxyvitamin D [25(OH)D], the primary circulating form, and the major metabolite used to determine a patient's vitamin D status (Holick, 2006). Secondly, in the kidneys, 25(OH)D is taken up by the proximal renal tubules through endocytic internalization mediated by megalin and cubilin, which are members of the LDL receptor family (Nykjaer et al, 1999). 
Subsequently, in the proximal renal tubules, 25(OH)D is hydroxylated by the enzyme 1α-hydroxylase (CYP27B1) to 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D [1,25(OH)₂D], the biologically active form of vitamin D, which exhibits simultaneously autocrine, paracrine and intracrine effects (DeLuca, 2004). The enzyme 1α-hydroxylase is also expressed extra-renally in at least 17 types of cells (Kidd, 2010), extensively documented on placenta (Gray et al, 1979) and immune cells, like monocytes and macrophages (Stoffels et al., 2007; Esteban et al, 2004). 1,25(OH)₂D exhibits simultaneously autocrine, paracrine and intracrine effects (Kidd, 2010). 
Vitamin D receptor (VDR), a member of the steroid/thyroid hormone nuclear receptors family with spontaneous presence in the cytosol, directly or indirectly affects many genes through transcriptional modifications (Strugnell & Deluca, 1997). Their importance is unequivocal, as 500 genes are estimated to be directly regulated by VDRs (Carleberg, 2003). VDR is present across more than 38 types of tissues, controlling vital genes related to oxidative stress, inflammation and chronic diseases (Haussler et al., 2008). Vitamin D exerts its effect by binding to VDR, which heterodimerizes with the retinoid receptor (RXR), forming a complex that controls transcription of certain target genes (Jones et al., 1998). Vitamin D binding protein (DBP) is a specific carrier of vitamin D and its metabolites, as almost all serum 25-hydroxyvitamin D is bound to circulating DBP. (Bouillon, 2001).  
It has been found that more than 200 genes are directly or indirectly regulated by 1,25(OH)₂D, controlling cellular proliferation, differentiation, apoptosis, and angiogenesis (Nagpal & Rathnachalam, 2005). While vitamin D is well known for its important function in bone health, it also significantly affects pancreatic beta cells, insulin sensitivity and secretion.
3.2 Vitamin D Mechanism of Action 
	In this section, we will explore the mechanisms implicated for vitamin D in major metabolic processes, particularly in the context of MetS. We highlight vitamin D importance in almost every pathophysiologic component of MetS, including insulin resistance, dyslipidemia, dysregulated immunity and hormonal balance. 
Vitamin D and Calcium 
Vitamin D’s regulation of calcium and phosphorus homeostasis is essential for bone health and normal mineralization. Vitamin D, 1,25(OH)₂D, mediates intestinal calcium absorption by activating of the synthesis of TRPV6 (a calcium channel) and facilitating transportation by tight-junction modification (Casadei et al., 2021; Mosali et al., 2014). Additionally, it increases renal tubular reabsorption of calcium, by enhancing the expression of calbindin and calcium transport in the distal convoluted tubule (Aguayo-Ruiz et al., 2020; Dusso et al., 2005).  Vitamin D deficiency, decreasing the availability of calcium, leads to structural defects that mimic osteoporosis (Levin et al., 1976). When administered, it effectively increases intestinal absorption of these important minerals and also promotes proper osteoblast function, further confirming its pleiotropic nature (Christakos et al, 2016). 
Parathyroid hormone (PTH) holds a primary role in regulating vitamin D activity by stimulating the transcription of 1αhydroxylase, thereby increasing production of 1,25(OH)₂D, which in turn provides negative feedback suppressing PTH synthesis and creating a loop (Christakos et al, 2010). Osteoblasts are activated by osteocalcin and PTH, which need vitamin D and K2 for proper function. PTH has also been implicated in insulin resistance, by reducing glucose uptake by liver, muscle and adipose cells (Thomas et al, 1995), while another study indicated that PTH decreased insulin-stimulated glucose uptake (Reusch et al, 1991).
VItamin D and Glucose 
Vitamin D is extensively involved in glucose metabolism, insulin production and insulin sensitivity, directly affecting the core metabolic dysfunction of MetS. A pivotal study by Ogunkolade et al. (2002) was one of the earliest works to demonstrate the association of vitamin D receptor polymorphism with insulin secretory capacity. Notably, 1,25-(OH)2D acts as an activator of the human insulin gene, playing an essential role in its transcription and subsequent excretion (Maestro et al, 2002). By acting directly on pancreatic beta cells and insulin-sensitive tissues, Vitamin D reduces insulin resistance, enhancing receptor expression and responsiveness (Pittas et al, 2007). 
The positive role of vitamin D is mediated by improved pancreatic β-cells function, including direct stimulation of insulin production and secretion through the extended presence of vitamin D receptors (VDR) (Johnson et al, 1994). Additionally, by regulating the intracellular and extracellular calcium pool of β-cells, vitamin D deficiency may disrupt normal release of insulin (Sooy et al, 1999). Calcium homeostasis is also influenced by vitamin K2, suggesting a collaborative effect. 
Vitamin D and Cardiovascular Health
Vitamin D affects lipid metabolism at the genetic level, being involved both in lipid synthesis and clearance. Adequate levels of Vitamin D are associated with favorable lipid profiles, including lower triglycerides (Jorde et al, 2010). A potential mechanism for increased lipogenesis associated with vitamin D deficiency is mediated by elevated PTH secretion, which stimulates lipogenesis by promoting an influx of calcium into the adipocytes (McCarty & Thomas, 2003). PTH and osteocalcin are also regulated by vitamin K2. Vitamin D deficiency leads to upregulation of renin, a mechanism implicated in Metabolic Syndrome hypertension, proving its regulatory role in the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS) (Li et al, 2002). 
Vitamin D and Inflammation
 Beyond bone health, vitamin D exhibits potent anti-inflammatory properties, influencing both the innate and adaptive immunity (Aranow, 2011). These anti-inflammatory effects are significant in the context of MetS, where low-grade inflammation is present. Additionally, vitamin D, found to affect proliferation and differentiation of cells, has been implicated in cancer prevention and CVD (Holcik & Chen, 2008). Vitamin D exhibits immune-modulating effects by downregulating pro-inflammatory cytokines and upregulating anti-inflammatory cytokines, reducing chronic inflammation in adipose tissue and, thus, regulating a key component of metabolic syndrome (Guillot, 2010). Moreover, vitamin D deficiency is connected to increased activity of PPARγ and C/EBPα receptors, components that are involved in the maturation of pre-adipocytes, leading to increased volume of adipose tissue ready to accumulate fat (Wood, 2008).

### 66. **Simos:**

now that's my final take on the introduction on vitamin K2. 

We need logical flow within mets, d and k2 

Chapter 4: VItamin K2 

4.1 Vitamin K2 Introduction 
Vitamin K occurs naturally under two main forms: phylloquinone (vitamin K1) and various menaquinones (collectively known as vitamin K2) (Mahdinia et al, 2019). It was first discovered in 1935 by Henrik Dam, who recognised the crucial role of vitamin K in blood clotting, after observing severe bleeding in chickens fed a low-fat diet (Dam, 1935). Vitamin K consists of a group of menadione derivatives, with phylloquinone (vitamin K1) being the the main form found mainly in green leafy vegetables (e.g., spinach, cabbage, kale), fruits (e.g., avocado, kiwi, grapes), as well as in some plant oils (e.g., soybean oil) (Halder et al., 2019).  
Menaquinones, structurally analogous to phylloquinone, were later discovered and collectively termed vitamin K2 (Zhao et al., 2021). Vitamin K2, being a fat soluble vitamin, has a lipophilic nature that allows its crossing through the blood brain barrier (BBB) (Talaei Firozjaei et al., 2018). Vitamin K2 has multiple forms, all sharing a common moiety in their chemical structure called menadione, which is a 2-methyl 1,4-naphthoquinone ring. They differ from each other to the degree of saturation and the length of side chains (Iwamoto et al., 2006). The repetitive unsaturated isoprene units of vitamin K2 provide strong rigidity and result in distinct cis- and trans-isomers, notably with trans-isomers being the only biologically active (Lal & Berenjian, 2020). Menaquinones are also referred to as MK-n, with 'n' denoting the length of its side chain varying from 4 to 15 isoprene units (Beulens et al., 2010). Each form of Vitamin K2 has distinct pharmacodynamic and pharmacokinetic properties, exhibiting unique potency and absorption efficiency (Booth & Rajabi, 2008). 
Vitamin K exhibits its action though the γ-carboxylation of vitamin K-dependent proteins (VKDPs) tied to cycles of oxidation and reduction (Oldenburg et al., 2008). The fully reduced forms of vitamin K (hydroquinone) donate their electrons to γ-glutamyl carboxylase (GGCX), activating the carboxylation of targeted glutamate groups belonging to specific proteins, and then the oxidized form is recycled back by vitamin K epoxide reductase (VKOR), using NADH as a cofactor (Kidd, 2010; Oldenburg et al., 2008). At least 17 essential "vitamin K dependent enzymes" have been found, such as osteocalcin and prothrombin, regulating blood coagulation, bone metabolism, and arterial calcification (Bleizgys, 2024; Cranenburg et al., 2007)
Vitamin K2 has two main sources: dietary intake from meat, dairy and fermented foods, and endogenous synthesis by probiotic gut bacteria (Shearer & Newman, 2008). Long-chain menaquinones (MK-7 to MK-13) are mainly synthesized by various bacteria including aerobic and anaerobic species, such as Escherichia, Lactococcus, Bacillus, Corynebacterium and Vibrio (Shea & Booth, 2016), some of which are considered part of the human intestinal microbiome (Walther et al., 2013). In bacteria menaquinones are used as electron carriers in the electron transport chain, sharing functions with ubiquinone (coQ10 enzyme) that also contains isoprenoid side chains of varying length (Marles et al., 2017). However, the amount of vitamin K2 produced by the intestinal microbiome does not meet the needs of the human body and is affected by the limited colonic absorption potential (Liu et al., 2019). Natto, a Japanese traditional food produced by fermentation of soybeans using Bacillus Subtilis, is found to have very high concentrations of MK-7 (Akbulut et al., 2020).
 3.2 Vitamin K2 Mechanism of Action 
	In this section, the mechanisms by which vitamin K2 influences metabolic processes will be explored, particularly in the context of MetS. The connections between MetS pathophysiology, vitamin D and vitamin K2 are highlighted, underscoring their potential synergistic effects. 
3.2.1 Vitamin K2 and Calcium
Vitamin K2 has been thoroughly supported for its crucial role in bone health, directly promoting the integration of calcium to the bones (Halder et al., 2019). Vitamin K dependent proteins’ function is hindered in the absence of vitamin K2, leading to pathological complications including metabolic conditions, like diabetes, and chronic degenerative diseases, like vascular calcification and osteoporosis (Vaidya et al., 2022b). These carboxylation activated the proteins, enabling calcium binding needed for their biological function. 
3.2.2 Vitamin K2 and Bone Metabolism 
Osteocalcin (OC) is a 49 amino acid peptide synthesized in osteoblasts and is the most abundant non-collagenous protein in bone, positively correlated with osteoblast function and differentiation. After post-translational modification the fully carboxylated (cOC) and partially carboxylated (uOC) forms are produced (Ducy et al., 1996). Vitamin K activates calcium binding in bones by carboxylating OC (forming γ-carboxyglutamate (Gla) residues), contributing to the creation of  hydroxyapatite crystals and preventing over-mineralization (Bleizgys, 2024). High levels of serum undercarboxylated osteocalcin or uncarboxylated osteocalcin reflects a low vitamin K2 status and high carboxylated osteocalcin reflects a high vitamin K status (Booth et al., 2013). Interestingly, Vitamin D has a major role in stimulating the production of osteocalcin in osteoblasts.Periostin is another VKDP occurring mainly in the extracellular matrix of collagen-rich connective tissues that plays a role in modeling of periodontal ligaments, trabecular bones, and heart valves (Kidd, 2010).  
3.2.3 Vitamin K2 and Glucose 
Undercarboxylated osteocalcin (ucOC) is an active hormone that affects glucose metabolism and is associated with diabetes (Lin et al., 2018), exerting metabolic effects within the pancreas and adipose tissue improving the sensitivity to insulin and improving glucose metabolism. It is related to increased synthesis and secretion of insulin in beta cells and stimulating the release of adiponectin from adipose tissue, improving insulin resistance and improving the overall glucose metabolism (Ferron et al., 2008).
3.2.4 Vitamin K2 and Cardiovascular Health 
Vitamin K2 has been widely linked to blood vessel wall health. Matrix Gla Protein (MGP) is another VKDP, operating mainly in the extracellular matrix of soft tissues, like vascules, heart, lung and cartilage, with  the role of down regulating calcium deposition (Proudfoot & Shanahan, 2006; Oldenburg et al., 2008). Multiple data suggest that vitamin K2 could potentially reduce the progression of vascular calcification and, thus, provide cardiovascular benefit (Casadei et al., 2021; Vossen et al., 2019). 
3.2.5 Vitamin K2 and Inflammation
Vitamin K2, particularly MK7 exerts anti-inflammatory effects by suppression of proinflammatory mediators such as IL-1α, IL-1β and TNF-α (Jadhav et al, 2022) and lipopolysaccharide-induced expression of inflammatory cytokines, mediated through various signal transduction pathways such as PI3K/ AKT, MAP Kinase, JAK/STAT, NF-κB, etc (Ohsaki et al, 2010). Chronic low-grade inflammation is a hallmark of MetS and vitamin D and vitamin K2, possessing potential anti-inflammatory effect, could provide targeted benefit.

### 69. **Simos:**

3.4 Studies on Vitamin D 
Vitamin D Levels and MetS 
There are several epidemiological studies that have identified a significant correlation between low levels of vitamin D and higher rates of metabolic syndrome or its components.  
Mitri et al. (2011), highlighted the seasonal variation in diabetes control, worsening during winter when vitamin D is in the lowest range, underscore a possible connection between glucose metabolism and vitamin D seasonal fluctuations. Additionally, Jorde et al.(2010) conducted a large population trial in Norway including data from 10229 subjects, which revealed an inverse association of 25(OH)D concentrations with BMI which is consistent in summer and winter months, indicating that seasonal variation does not interfere with this relationship.
In a significant population-based study, Song et al (2013) included about 70000 participants and described a reduction of 38% in relative risk of diabetes incidence for individuals with the highest serum vitamin D levels, compared to those with the lowest. Specifically, a 4% reduction in T2DM risk was observed for each increment of 4 ng/mL (10 nmol/mL) in serum vitamin D levels. 
Furthermore, Fite et al. (2020) showcased in their hallmark study involving the U.S. non-institutionalized civilian population of 10,348 people that vitamin D deficiency is  significantly associated with MetS across all ages and reported a significant association between vitamin D deficiency and components of MetS, including triglycerides (TG), HDL, fasting blood sugar (FBS), IR, blood pressure (BP), and waist circumference (WC).
Vitamin D Levels and Adiposity Measures
	Increased adiposity and obesity are also connected to low vitamin D levels, affected by large distribution volume and affecting the progress of MetS. Cheng et al (2010) observed an inverse association between serum vitamin D concentrations and measure of adiposity in 1882 subjects,with the prevalence of vitamin D deficiency being three times higher in individuals with a higher fat concentration. Similarly, González et al. (2015) confirmed in a clinic based sample that those with higher BMI, waist circumference, and waist-to-height ratio had a significantly lower vitamin D status. Lenders et  al. (2009) noted that in adolescents "a decrease of 0.5 ng/mL of 25(OH)D in adolescents was related to 1% increment in  body fat mass". These findings are also consistent in studies of children population, where low vitamin D intake is correlated to higher body weight, BMI, waist circumference and waist/hip ratio (Rodríguez-Rodríguez et al, 2010; Alemzadeh et al, 2008).
Vitamin D in Specific Populations
	The relationship between vitamin D levels and metabolic syndrome is further elucidated and supported by research performed in specific demographic groups. 
Huang et al. (2015) studied non-diabetic and young individuals, revealing an inverse relationship between vitamin D levels and Metabolic Syndrome.
 In the older population of Korean men and women over 65 years old, Lee et al. (2019) found that lower vitamin D levels were associated with an increased prevalence of MetS, noted in elevated WC, TG and LDL cholesterol. 
Mirhoseini et al. (2018), studying 192 obese patients from two hospitals in  Iran's third-largest city, found that TG, HDL-C, FBS, and BP were significantly associated with vitamin D levels (Mirhoseini et al, 2018). 
Barbalho et al. (2018) studying patients  in a cardiology unit found that 80% had vitamin D deficiency and that all of the patients with hypovitaminosis D had MetS, also observing significantly higher levels of glycemia, glycosylated hemoglobin, total cholesterol, LDLs, triglycerides, and atherogenic indices and an elevated BMI.
In a study of patients in intensive care in Australia, 17% had undetectable serum 25D levels, and 50% were deficient or insufficient (serum 25D <24 ng/mL), calculating that the mortality rate was three times higher in those with levels <24 ng/mL compared to those with sufficient levels (>60 ng/mL) (Lee et al., 2009).
Vitamin D Meta-Analyses and Prospective Studies
	Meta-analyses and prospective studies hold the potential of providing insights regarding the broad and long-term effects of vitamin D on MetS. Multiple studies have provided insights for the beneficial role of vitamin D in MetS. 
Ju et al. (2014) conducted a dose-response meta-analysis on blood vitamin D status and in the general adult population and concluded that "a 25 nmol/L increase in 25(OH)D levels was associated with a 13% decrease in the risk of metabolic syndrome in cross-sectional studies". 
Zhu and Heil (2018) observed a linear relationship between 25(OH)D concentrations and serum concentrations of glucose and lipids, calculating that each increase of 1 ng/mL 25(OH)D was associated with a significant reduction in total cholesterol and LDL and decreased risk of MetS. Krishnaswamy et al. (2019) in a cross-sectional study with 80 Indians aged between 18 and 60 years were found that Vit D  level in the Metabolic Syndrome was significantly lower than that in the healthy group. 
Hajhashemy et al (2021) performed a large systematic review and dose–response meta‐analysis of epidemiologic studies on serum vitamin D levels in relation to MetS. They successfully included 43 studies in the analysis, including 298,187 general adult population and concluded that "the highest level of  serum vitamin D, compared with the lowest level, was significantly related to a 43%  decreased odds of MetS in developed countries and 40% in developing countries.” Additionally, they revealed that for each 25 nmol/L increase in serum vitamin D level there were significantly  decreased odds of MetS by 15%.
A Danish long-term prospective study with a median of 20 years follow up, involving 9841 participants from “The Copenhagen City Heart Study”, reported that "for 25(OH)D less than 12.5 nmol/L compared with levels more than 50 nmol/L, the HR for Type 2 Diabetes Melitus (T2DM) was 1.22, and was 1.35 for lowest compared with highest quartile" (Afzal et al., 2013). 
Parker et al (2010) performed their systemic review and meta-analysis on levels of vitamin D and cardiometabolic disorders. Including 99,745 participants they found that "the highest levels of serum 25OHD were associated with a 43% reduction in cardiometabolic disorders. Specifically, they reported "a significant association between high levels of vitamin D and a reduction in the risk of having cardiovascular disease (33% reduction compared to low levels of vitamin D), type 2 diabetes (55% reduction) and metabolic syndrome (51% reduction)". 
  
Vitamin D Clinical Trials
Vitamin D and Adiposity 

	Finally, multiple recent clinical trials provide strong evidence for the important role and potential therapeutic action of vitamin D in MetS, its components and resulting clinical manifestations.
 Dong et al (2010), in a pivotal 16-week randomized clinical trial using 2000 international units daily vitamin D3 supplementation, showed that a higher vitamin D serum status was contributing to decreased adiposity by inhibiting the differentiation of pre-adipocytes.This finding suggest that vitamin D may have a critical role in modulating adipose tissue, preventing the further expansion of fat mass.
Additionally, Salehpour et al. (2012) found that supplementing with vitamin D3 (25 μg/day as cholecalciferol) led to decreases in body fat mass in obese subjects with vitamin D as compared to obese subjects with placebo, without corresponding changed in weight and waist circumference, noting its selective effect in modulating adipose tissue physiology. 
Saliba et al. (2013) provided an interesting perspective and concluded that BMI was inversely associated with the increase in  serum Vitamin D levels after supplementation, highlighting the complex interactions affected by the large distribution volume. 

Vitamin D Effects on Glucose Metabolism and Insulin Sensitivity
	 Vitamin D plays a major role in physiology of insulin production and glucose metabolism, with its impact being particularly noted in clinical trials. 
Lemieux et al. (2019) showed that supplementing with 5000 IU daily of vitamin D significantly increased insulin sensitivity and the activity of pancreatic β-cells. Safarpour et al (2020) used supplementation with 50,000 IU/week of vitamin D for 8 weeks in patients with type 2 diabetes and found a significant reduction in glycosylated hemoglobin and an increase in sirtuin 1, which appears related to β-cells insulin production. 

Vitamin D Effects on Lipid Profiles and Inflammatory Markers
The modulation of dyslipidemia and inflammation has also been documented in clinical trials using vitamin D supplementation. 
Zitterman et al (2009) showed that vitamin D supplementation lead to decreases in PTH (-26.5%), triglycerides (-13.5%) and inflammatory markers, such as the tumor necrosis factor (TNF-α) (-10.2%) compared to the placebo. Further reinforcing these findings,
 Jamilian et al. (2017), using supplementation with 50,000 IU every 2 weeks of vitamin D for 24 weeks, observed improvements in TGs and VLDL. 
Gadheri et al. (2017) also observed significant reduction in TGs and LDL even after a shorter period of 12 weeks.
 Vitamin D Studies Reporting No Benefit
While most of the studies highlight the benefit of vitamin D in various metabolic dysfunctions and diseases, some evidence suggest no benefit, while in rare occasions even potential harm has been reported. This chapter reviews some of the trials considered well-structured to provide a more complete view on the subject. 
In Asian Indians, Majumdar et al. (2001),  contrary to previous findings, found that vitamin D administration did not affect insulin resistance in prediabetic patients, proposing a variating possible impact among different ethnic backgrounds. In another study by Botella-Carretero et al. (2007),  vitamin D deficiency was found to be associated with metabolic syn

### 71. **ChatGPT:**

Below are suggestions for converting your current “list of studies” format into a more cohesive narrative. We’ll use your Vitamin D section as an example, then outline a similar approach you can apply to the K2 chapter. This involves adding transitions, synthesizing findings rather than just listing them, and arranging studies into a logical sequence that tells a story. After the guidelines, you’ll find a sample revised paragraph using some of your cited studies.

---

### General Strategies for Creating a Narrative

1. **Group Studies by Theme or Outcome:**  
   Instead of reporting studies one by one in isolation, cluster them by their main finding or relevance. For example, start broadly with population-level correlations (epidemiological data), then narrow down to specific populations, followed by mechanistic insights and intervention studies (clinical trials).

2. **Use Transitional Phrases and Sentences:**  
   Introduce each group of studies by setting the context. For example, “Epidemiological evidence strongly supports a link between low vitamin D status and MetS,” then present a few key studies as examples. Next, transition to a different angle: “Beyond general populations, certain demographic groups may experience even stronger associations…”

3. **Synthesize and Compare Findings:**  
   Rather than repeating the methods and participant numbers of each study, emphasize what the studies collectively suggest. For instance, if several studies show an inverse relationship between vitamin D levels and adiposity, highlight that consensus. If one study differs (e.g., no benefit or harm), note it as an exception and discuss potential reasons.

4. **Contextualize Findings in MetS Pathophysiology:**  
   Always link study outcomes back to MetS components. For example, after discussing how vitamin D correlates with lower BMI or improved glucose control, remind the reader that these improvements address core MetS abnormalities.

5. **Acknowledge Inconsistencies or Limitations:**  
   When some studies show no benefit or even negative effects, integrate these findings by noting differing population characteristics, baseline levels of vitamin D, or duration and dose of supplementation.

6. **Foreshadow Synergy with Other Nutrients (Such as K2):**  
   While you’re focusing on vitamin D studies here, you can insert a sentence that these findings lay the groundwork for examining how vitamin D’s actions might be complemented by other micronutrients like vitamin K2.

---

### Example Revision of Your Vitamin D Studies Section into a Narrative

**Original (Excerpt):**  
“There are several epidemiological studies that have identified a significant correlation between low levels of vitamin D and higher rates of metabolic syndrome or its components. Mitri et al. (2011)… Jorde et al.(2010)… Song et al (2013)…”

**Revised Narrative Paragraph:**

*“A growing body of epidemiological evidence supports the association between low serum vitamin D levels and the development of MetS. Large-scale population studies demonstrate that individuals with lower vitamin D concentrations are more prone to insulin resistance, dyslipidemia, and central obesity—core components of MetS. For instance, Mitri et al. (2011) highlighted that diabetes control worsens during winter, when vitamin D levels tend to drop, suggesting seasonal fluctuations in vitamin D may influence glucose metabolism. Similarly, Jorde et al. (2010), analyzing data from over 10,000 subjects in Norway, found an inverse relationship between 25(OH)D levels and BMI that persisted throughout the year, indicating that vitamin D status could have stable, long-term effects on adiposity. Further reinforcing these findings, Song et al. (2013) demonstrated that individuals with the highest serum vitamin D levels had a 38% reduced relative risk of incident T2DM compared to those with the lowest levels, translating into a 4% risk reduction per 4 ng/mL increment of serum vitamin D. Collectively, these studies highlight that maintaining adequate vitamin D status may mitigate several metabolic disturbances, thus lowering MetS risk.”*

**Continuing the Narrative with Other Themes:**

After this initial overview, you might introduce studies in specific populations or discuss adiposity measures:

*“This inverse association between vitamin D and MetS risk extends across diverse demographic groups and adiposity measures. For example, Cheng et al. (2010) reported that vitamin D deficiency was three times more common in individuals with higher fat concentration, while González et al. (2015) corroborated these findings by showing that subjects with greater BMI and waist-to-height ratios had significantly lower vitamin D levels. Even in younger populations, similar trends emerge. Lenders et al. (2009) found that a mere 0.5 ng/mL reduction in 25(OH)D among adolescents corresponded to a 1% increase in body fat mass, underscoring vitamin D’s influence on adiposity from early life. Such data suggest that interventions aimed at optimizing vitamin D could be beneficial at various life stages and degrees of adiposity.”*

You would follow a similar pattern when you talk about vitamin D in specific ethnic or age groups, referencing the studies by Huang et al. (2015), Lee et al. (2019), Mirhoseini et al. (2018), and Barbalho et al. (2018). For each cluster, start with a general statement (“In specific demographic subsets, vitamin D’s role becomes even clearer…”) then cite a few representative studies, and conclude by tying their findings back to MetS pathophysiology.

For meta-analyses and clinical trials, introduce them after the observational data, stating something like:  
*“Beyond cross-sectional and observational data, meta-analyses and prospective studies provide a broader and more rigorous perspective on vitamin D’s influence on MetS. Ju et al. (2014), for example, demonstrated… Meanwhile, randomized clinical trials by Dong et al. (2010) and Salehpour et al. (2012) highlight the potential therapeutic action of vitamin D supplementation in reducing adiposity and improving insulin sensitivity.”*

When addressing studies showing no benefit or potential harm, integrate them by acknowledging complexity and heterogeneity:  
*“Despite the generally positive trends, not all studies have found improvements. Trials in certain ethnic groups or using varying doses and durations of supplementation yielded negligible effects on insulin resistance (Majumdar et al., 2001; Botella-Carretero et al., 2007) or diabetes prevention (de Boer et al., 2008; Talari et al., 2019). These mixed outcomes underscore the importance of baseline vitamin D status, population genetics, and metabolic background in determining responsiveness. Such discrepancies emphasize the need for personalized approaches and further exploration into nutrient-nutrient interactions, such as the synergy between vitamin D and vitamin K2, to fully understand how to optimize micronutrient interventions for MetS.”*

### 3.4 Studies on Vitamin D

A substantial body of epidemiological, observational, and interventional research supports the link between vitamin D status and the risk, prevalence, and severity of metabolic syndrome (MetS) and its individual components. These studies encompass various populations, age groups, and geographic settings, collectively reinforcing the concept that adequate vitamin D levels may exert protective metabolic effects. While most findings suggest beneficial associations, some investigations have noted negligible or even negative outcomes, underscoring the complexity of vitamin D’s role and the influence of factors such as ethnicity, baseline vitamin D status, dosage, and duration of supplementation.

#### Vitamin D Levels and MetS

Epidemiological evidence strongly suggests that lower serum vitamin D levels correlate with higher rates of MetS or its components. For instance, Mitri et al. (2011) demonstrated that diabetes control deteriorates during winter, when vitamin D is at its lowest, highlighting how seasonal fluctuations in vitamin D might affect glucose metabolism. Similarly, Jorde et al. (2010), analyzing data from 10,229 subjects in Norway, found an inverse association between 25(OH)D concentrations and BMI. Notably, this relationship held true in both summer and winter, indicating that seasonal changes do not significantly interfere with vitamin D’s link to adiposity.

Reinforcing these observations, Song et al. (2013), in a large population-based study of approximately 70,000 participants, reported that individuals with the highest serum vitamin D levels had a 38% reduction in the relative risk of diabetes incidence compared to those with the lowest levels. They also noted a 4% reduction in T2DM risk per each 4 ng/mL (10 nmol/mL) increment in serum vitamin D. Further support comes from Fite et al. (2020), who examined 10,348 U.S. non-institutionalized civilians and found significant associations between vitamin D deficiency and MetS, as well as its components—including triglycerides (TG), HDL, fasting blood sugar (FBS), insulin resistance (IR), blood pressure (BP), and waist circumference (WC).

#### Vitamin D Levels and Adiposity Measures

Obesity and increased adiposity, key drivers of MetS, are also closely linked to vitamin D status. Cheng et al. (2010) observed an inverse association between serum vitamin D concentrations and adiposity in 1,882 subjects, noting a threefold higher prevalence of vitamin D deficiency among those with greater fat mass. González et al. (2015) confirmed these findings in a clinic-based sample, showing that individuals with higher BMI, WC, and waist-to-height ratios exhibited significantly lower vitamin D levels. Lenders et al. (2009) extended this relationship to adolescents, reporting that a decrease of 0.5 ng/mL in 25(OH)D was associated with a 1% increase in body fat mass. Similar trends are observed in children, as studies by Rodríguez-Rodríguez et al. (2010) and Alemzadeh et al. (2008) correlate low vitamin D intake and status with higher body weight, BMI, WC, and waist/hip ratio in younger populations. Collectively, these findings suggest that optimizing vitamin D status may beneficially influence body composition and thus mitigate a core aspect of MetS.

#### Vitamin D in Specific Populations

Exploring different demographic groups further clarifies vitamin D’s role in MetS. Huang et al. (2015) reported an inverse relationship between vitamin D levels and MetS in young, non-diabetic individuals, indicating that vitamin D-related metabolic benefits may manifest early in adulthood. Among older Korean men and women aged over 65, Lee et al. (2019) found that lower vitamin D levels correlated with higher prevalence of MetS—particularly elevated WC, TG, and LDL cholesterol—demonstrating that adequate vitamin D may remain crucial in older populations. Mirhoseini et al. (2018), studying 192 obese patients in Iran, observed significant associations between vitamin D and several MetS components (TG, HDL-C, FBS, and BP). Similarly, Barbalho et al. (2018), investigating patients in a cardiology unit, found that 80% had vitamin D deficiency and all patients with hypovitaminosis D presented with MetS and significantly worse glycemia, HbA1c, total cholesterol, LDL, TG, and atherogenic indices, as well as elevated BMI. Even in acute care settings, such as an Australian intensive care unit, Lee et al. (2009) documented that 17% of patients had undetectable serum 25D levels and 50% were deficient or insufficient (<24 ng/mL), with mortality rates three times higher in those with severely low vitamin D compared to those with sufficient levels (>60 ng/mL). These population-specific findings underscore the potential broad impact of vitamin D on metabolic health across diverse age groups and clinical settings.

#### Vitamin D Meta-Analyses and Prospective Studies

Meta-analyses and prospective longitudinal studies provide a broader and more rigorous perspective on vitamin D’s metabolic implications. Ju et al. (2014), in a dose-response meta-analysis, concluded that a 25 nmol/L increase in 25(OH)D levels was associated with a 13% decrease in MetS risk in cross-sectional studies. Zhu and Heil (2018) found a linear relationship between 25(OH)D and improvements in glucose and lipid profiles, noting that each 1 ng/mL increase in 25(OH)D was linked to significant reductions in total cholesterol and LDL, and consequently a decreased risk of MetS. Krishnaswamy et al. (2019), in a cross-sectional study of 80 Indian adults (18–60 years), reported lower vitamin D levels in individuals with MetS compared to healthy controls.

Hajhashemy et al. (2021), conducting a large systematic review and dose–response meta-analysis of 43 studies (298,187 individuals), concluded that the highest vitamin D levels, compared to the lowest, corresponded to a 43% decreased odds of MetS in developed countries and 40% in developing countries. They further noted a 15% decrease in MetS odds for each 25 nmol/L increase in serum vitamin D. Complementing these findings, a Danish prospective study from “The Copenhagen City Heart Study” (Afzal et al., 2013), with a median of 20 years of follow-up in 9,841 participants, reported that individuals with 25(OH)D less than 12.5 nmol/L had a hazard ratio (HR) of 1.22 for T2DM compared to those above 50 nmol/L, and an HR of 1.35 comparing the lowest vs. highest quartiles of vitamin D. Parker et al. (2010), examining 99,745 participants in a systematic review and meta-analysis, found that the highest 25OHD levels corresponded to a 43% reduction in cardiometabolic disorders, including a 33% reduction in cardiovascular disease risk, a 55% reduction in T2DM risk, and a 51% reduction in MetS prevalence.

#### Vitamin D Clinical Trials: Adiposity, Glucose Metabolism, and Lipid Profiles

Randomized clinical trials add another layer of evidence, examining whether supplementation can improve adiposity, glycemic control, and lipid/inflammatory markers. Dong et al. (2010), in a pivotal 16-week trial using 2,000 IU/day vitamin D3, showed that higher serum vitamin D status contributed to decreased adiposity by inhibiting pre-adipocyte differentiation, suggesting vitamin D’s potential in modulating fat mass expansion. Salehpour et al. (2012) similarly noted that 25 μg/day (1,000 IU/day) cholecalciferol supplementation decreased body fat mass in obese subjects compared to placebo, without changes in weight or WC, underscoring selective effects on adipose tissue physiology. Saliba et al. (2013) concluded that BMI was inversely associated with increases in serum vitamin D after supplementation, highlighting the interplay between distribution volume and vitamin D status.

Focusing on glucose metabolism, Lemieux et al. (2019) demonstrated that 5,000 IU/day vitamin D supplementation significantly enhanced insulin sensitivity and pancreatic β-cell activity, while Safarpour et al. (2020) showed that 50,000 IU/week vitamin D for 8 weeks in T2DM patients lowered HbA1c and increased sirtuin 1, related to β-cell insulin production.

Regarding lipid profiles and inflammatory markers, Zitterman et al. (2009) reported that vitamin D supplementation reduced PTH by 26.5%, TG by 13.5%, and TNF-α by 10.2%, improving both metabolic and inflammatory parameters. Jamilian et al. (2017), using 50,000 IU every two weeks for 24 weeks, observed improvements in TG and VLDL, while Gadheri et al. (2017) noted significant TG and LDL reductions after just 12 weeks of supplementation, reinforcing vitamin D’s multifaceted role in MetS management.

#### Vitamin D Studies Reporting No Benefit

Despite the generally positive trends, not all studies have found significant improvements, and a few have even hinted at possible harm under certain conditions. In Asian Indians, Majumdar et al. (2001) noted no effect of vitamin D administration on insulin resistance in prediabetic patients, suggesting ethnic or genetic differences in vitamin D responsiveness. Botella-Carretero et al. (2007) associated vitamin D deficiency with MetS in morbid obesity but found that supplementation did not improve metabolic markers. Over seven years, de Boer et al. (2008) saw no preventive benefit against diabetes with 400 IU/day vitamin D3 and 1,000 mg calcium. Talari et al. (2019) found no advantage in supplementing vitamin D combined with high-dose omega-3 fatty acids, and a study by Davidson et al. (2013) using high-dose vitamin D3 (targeting 25(OH)D levels of 150–225 nmol/L) failed to reduce diabetes incidence. Moreover, 

*[Message truncated - 913 additional characters]*

3.4 Studies on Vitamin K2 
Vitamin K2 Animal Studies 
Human studies are currently limited on vitamin K2, making animal studies important to report and study, as they provide strong evidence of mechanistic action and potential benefits in humans. 
Even as early as 1999, Sakamoto et al. (1999) observed that vitamin K deficiency led to delayed  insulin response and prolonged increase in plasma glucose, suggesting its role in glucose metabolism. More recently, Hussein et al. (2018) found that administration of high doses of vitamin K2 (MK-4) to diabetic rats over 8 weeks "resulted in a significant dose-dependent alleviation of HbA1c, fasting plasma glucose (FPG), homeostatic model assessment insulin resistance index (HOMA-IR)." Similarly, Iwamoto et al. (2011) had already shown that "diabetic rats fed a diet of 30 mg/kg of vitamin K2 (MK-4) 5 days a week for 12 weeks had decreased blood glucose (BG) and hyperglycemia." Finally, the effects of MK-4 and VK1 on glucose metabolism/homeostasis have also been found beneficial for  streptozotocin (STZ)-Type 1 diabetic rat models (Varsha et al, 2015). 
Osteocalcin concentrations have been described as a possible mediating mechanism, as its concentration affects insulin sensitivity by regulating the expression of insulin genes and cell proliferation factors (Ferron et al. 2008). Khalil et al.(2014) demonstrated significant decreases in levels of glucose, while insulin and adiponectin levels were increased, in ovariectomised exercised and non-exercised rats treated with MK-7, providing an even broader perspective of its metabolic benefits, but also its connection to vitamin D and MetS. 

Vitamin K2 Observational 
	In most human studies, the impact of vitamin K2 is stressed, largely differentiating its role from vitamin K1, even if high intakes of both phylloquinone and menaquinones have also been related to improved insulin sensitivity (Ibarrola-Jurado et al, 2012) and a favorable lipid profile (Braam et al, 2004). The lack of correlation between K1 and metabolic syndrome, was supported by the Rotterdam study, in which 4807 participants free from myocardial infarction at baseline were followed over a 7-year period, concluding that K1 did not impact the outcome significantly. Dam et al (2015), in a decade-long study that included 800 men and women (40-80y), found a decreased occurrence of MetS (lower TG and WC) in those with high vitamin K2 intake, without observing such a correlation with K1. 
In their study, Helmy et al (2020) included 60 T2-D patients and 30 non-diabetic controls, finding that serum VK2 level was significantly lower in diabetics, especially in those with uncontrolled hyperglycemia. Beulens et al. (2010) studied the associations of dietary K1 and K2 intakes with the risk of T2DM in a sample of 38,094  Dutch men and women (20–70 y) for an average 10.3 years, observing a reduced risk of type 2 diabetes with a hazard ratio (HR) of 0.81 for the highest versus the lowest quartiles. Their final multivariate model showed 7% T2DM risk reduction with each 10-μg increment of vitamin K2. In 2016, Manna et al., integrating the available evidence in their review, proposed that both forms of vitamin K reduced the risk of T2DM. 
Gast et al (2009), using data from the Prospect—The European Prevalence of Infection in Intensive Care (EPIC) study in the Netherlands, which followed 16,057 women between 49 and 70 years with no previous history of CVD was followed for 8.1 years, found a significant negative relationship between VK2 intake and  reduced risk of CVD, even after adjusting for known risk and dietary factors.In a recent prospective cohort study involving 53,372 participants with a follow-up of 17–22 years, finding that dietary intake of both K1 and K2 were inversely related to atherosclerotic CVD hospitalization risk (Bellinge et al, 2021). 
 
Vitamin K2 Clinical Trials 
Yoshida et al (2008) in a clinical trial including K2 supplementation for 4 weeks, found that it resulted in improved glucose tolerance and insulin resistance, possibly related to increased  cOC. Choi et al. (2011) further supported the relation between insulin sensitivity and vitamin K2 supplementation, in a clinical trial where 30mg of MK4 were supplemented healthy young men, as indicated by the improvements recorded only in the intervention group in insulin sensitivity index (P= 0.01) and disposition index (P < 0.01).  
Sakamoto et al (2000) studying the possible effects of one week vitamin K (menaquinone-4) tablets intake on glucose tolerance among young men with a low risk of diabetes, found delayed the insulin response and decreased plasma glucose. Naiyarakseree et al (2023) in a recent open-label, multicentre randomized controlled study including diabetics with kidney disease, concluded that MK-7 supplementation (375 μg/d, 24 weeks) lowered the rate of progression of arterial stiffness in chronic haemodialysis patients
Li et al (2018) observed significant changes in insulin concentration and sensitivity, focusing on MK4 and MK7, administered at mg and μg dose levels respectively, due to different pharmacokinetic properties. Finally, Kawana et al (2001) showed that ust 10 μg/day of K2 decreases diabetes risk by 7%.   
Zhao et al. (2024) in their recent systematic review and meta-analysis, including 17 studies, showed that vitamin K2 supplementation can improve insulin resistance, by reducing homeostatic model assessment insulin resistance (HOMA-IR). 
Vitamin K2 Studies Reporting No Benefit 
As expected, and similarly to vitamin D, certain studies have shown that supplementation of vitamin K2 is not always beneficial to cardiovascular disease or insulin resistance, especially in conditions like diabetes and aortic stenosis. This further highlights the need for a personalized approach for further research.
Studying the effects of the intake of MK-7 (360 μg/d) in a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled trial on insulin resistance, Rahimi Sakak et al. (2021) found no significant improvement in ameliorating insulin resistance in type 2 diabetes patients. Similarly, Suksomboon et al. (2017), in a meta-analysis studying both vitamin K1 and K2 (MK-4 and MK-7 subtypes), found that vitamin K supplementation did not affect insulin sensitivity. Additionally,  Meer et al. (2023) conducting a study with six months of supplementation with vitamin K2 (360 μg of MK-7) among type 2 diabetes patients, found no improvement in serum calcification propensity time.

### 3.4 Studies on Vitamin K2

Compared to vitamin D, the human research base on vitamin K2 remains more limited, making animal models and mechanistic studies particularly valuable. Nonetheless, existing human observational and clinical research strongly suggests that vitamin K2 influences several metabolic pathways relevant to MetS, including glucose homeostasis, cardiovascular health, and possibly adiposity and inflammation. While many studies point toward beneficial metabolic effects, some findings are mixed or show no improvement, mirroring the complexity observed with vitamin D research. Understanding vitamin K2’s role, both independently and in synergy with vitamin D, may offer new avenues for managing MetS.

#### Vitamin K2 Animal Studies

Animal studies have provided foundational insights into vitamin K2’s mechanistic actions on glucose metabolism and insulin sensitivity. Early work by Sakamoto et al. (1999) demonstrated that vitamin K deficiency led to delayed insulin responses and prolonged hyperglycemia in experimental models, suggesting a key role for K vitamins in regulating glucose homeostasis. More recent investigations have reinforced these findings. For example, Hussein et al. (2018) administered high doses of MK-4 (a form of vitamin K2) to diabetic rats for 8 weeks and observed dose-dependent improvements in HbA1c, fasting plasma glucose (FPG), and homeostatic model assessment insulin resistance index (HOMA-IR). Similarly, Iwamoto et al. (2011) showed that diabetic rats fed 30 mg/kg of MK-4, five days a week for 12 weeks, exhibited reductions in blood glucose (BG) and hyperglycemia. The benefits also extend to type 1 diabetic models: Varsha et al. (2015) reported that both MK-4 and vitamin K1 improved glucose metabolism in streptozotocin (STZ)-induced diabetic rats.

A potential mediator of these effects is osteocalcin, a vitamin K-dependent protein. Ferron et al. (2008) highlighted osteocalcin’s critical role in insulin sensitivity by modulating insulin gene expression and cell proliferation. Khalil et al. (2014) further expanded this perspective, demonstrating that in ovariectomized rats—both exercised and sedentary—treatment with MK-7 improved glucose parameters, increased insulin and adiponectin levels, and offered metabolic benefits that parallel those seen with vitamin D. These animal findings, while preliminary, set the stage for human studies and strengthen the rationale that vitamin K2, often in interplay with vitamin D, could influence metabolic pathways central to MetS.

#### Vitamin K2 Observational Studies

Human observational research, though more limited than that for vitamin D, also underscores vitamin K2’s metabolic importance, often distinguishing it from vitamin K1. While phylloquinone (K1) and menaquinones (K2) have both been associated with improved insulin sensitivity (Ibarrola-Jurado et al., 2012) and better lipid profiles (Braam et al., 2004), the impact on MetS seems more closely tied to K2. The Rotterdam study, following 4,807 participants free from myocardial infarction for seven years, found that K1 intake did not significantly influence MetS outcomes, suggesting that K2 may have distinct metabolic effects. Consistent with this notion, Dam et al. (2015) reported that in an 800-participant, decade-long study of adults aged 40–80, higher vitamin K2 intake correlated with lower triglycerides (TG) and waist circumference (WC), indicating a reduced occurrence of MetS. No similar relationship emerged for K1 intake, highlighting K2’s unique role.

Other observational findings support this K2-MetS connection. Helmy et al. (2020) noted that among 60 T2DM patients and 30 controls, serum K2 levels were significantly lower in diabetics, especially those with poor glycemic control. Large-scale prospective data also align with these patterns: Beulens et al. (2010), studying 38,094 Dutch adults over 10.3 years, found that each 10-μg increment in vitamin K2 intake reduced T2DM risk by 7%. Manna et al. (2016) integrated existing evidence, proposing that both K1 and K2 may reduce T2DM risk, though the relationship appears stronger and more direct for K2.

Beyond glucose metabolism, vitamin K2’s role extends to cardiovascular outcomes. Gast et al. (2009), using data from the Prospect-EPIC study of 16,057 women aged 49–70 followed for 8.1 years, identified a significant negative relationship between K2 intake and CVD risk—an effect that persisted even after controlling for known risk and dietary factors. More recently, Bellinge et al. (2021) examined 53,372 participants over 17–22 years and found that both K1 and K2 intake were inversely related to atherosclerotic CVD hospitalization risk, suggesting that K2’s vascular benefits may contribute to the reduced cardiometabolic burdens characteristic of MetS.

#### Vitamin K2 Clinical Trials

Although human observational studies suggest beneficial associations, clinical trials are crucial to test causality and intervention effectiveness. Yoshida et al. (2008) conducted a clinical trial supplementing vitamin K2 for four weeks and observed improved glucose tolerance and insulin resistance, potentially mediated by increased carboxylated osteocalcin (cOC). Choi et al. (2011) similarly supported K2’s insulin-sensitizing effects, showing that healthy young men receiving 30 mg of MK-4 exhibited significant improvements in insulin sensitivity and disposition indices.

Short-term interventions also yield positive metabolic insights. Sakamoto et al. (2000) found that just one week of menaquinone-4 supplementation improved glucose tolerance in young men with low diabetes risk. Additionally, Naiyarakseree et al. (2023), in an open-label, multicenter randomized controlled study in diabetic patients with kidney disease, reported that 24 weeks of MK-7 supplementation (375 μg/day) slowed the progression of arterial stiffness, a key cardiovascular concern in MetS. Li et al. (2018) noted that MK-4 and MK-7 have differing pharmacokinetics, yet both significantly affected insulin concentration and sensitivity. Even low doses may matter: Kawana et al. (2001) showed that a mere 10 μg/day of K2 can decrease diabetes risk by 7%. Complementing these trial results, Zhao et al. (2024) conducted a systematic review and meta-analysis of 17 studies, concluding that K2 supplementation can improve insulin resistance, evidenced by reductions in HOMA-IR.

#### Vitamin K2 Studies Reporting No Benefit

Despite these promising results, not all K2 supplementation trials have produced favorable outcomes. Similar to vitamin D, certain populations and conditions appear less responsive. For instance, Rahimi Sakak et al. (2021) found that administering 360 μg/day of MK-7 did not significantly improve insulin resistance in type 2 diabetes patients during a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled trial. Suksomboon et al. (2017), performing a meta-analysis on both K1 and K2 (MK-4 and MK-7), reported no notable improvements in insulin sensitivity. Additionally, Meer et al. (2023), studying type 2 diabetes patients over six months of 360 μg/day MK-7 supplementation, observed no improvement in serum calcification propensity time, suggesting no beneficial impact on vascular calcification in this cohort.

These divergent findings underscore the complexity of vitamin K2’s metabolic effects, indicating that outcomes may depend on baseline vitamin K2 levels, genetic background, concurrent micronutrient status (including vitamin D), or disease severity. Such discrepancies highlight the necessity for personalized approaches and further investigation to determine which subgroups benefit most from K2 interventions.

---

### Conclusion

Taken together, the evidence surrounding vitamin K2’s role in metabolic health is evolving, with animal studies providing strong mechanistic bases and human research—both observational and interventional—generally pointing toward favorable effects on glucose metabolism and cardiovascular parameters. Nonetheless, mixed findings remind us that the metabolic landscape is intricate, shaped by individual variability and potentially moderated by other nutrients like vitamin D. As research advances, more precise dosing, longer trial durations, and tailored supplementation strategies may clarify the full potential of vitamin K2 in the management of MetS.

now let;s kinda do the same for the last and most important chapter of the article. 

we have been building this troughout

Chapter 4: The Synergy of Vitamins D and K2 on Metabolic Syndrome and Its Components
4.1 Introduction 
Vitamin D and vitamin K2 could be perceived as pleiotropic co-factors due to the diversity of their common mechanisms of action and their implication in multiple clinical syndromes, as their classic vitamin role undermines their importance. Multiple studies have already presented that combinational supplementation of vitamin D and vitamin K2 provides important and favorable effects when compared to single nutrient supplementation (Singh et al., 2022).
4.2 Synergy Definition and Nutritional Approaches  
Nutrient synergy is defined as the resulting physiological effect arising from the combinational action of two or more molecules, and is greater than the effect of each molecule separately (Davidson & Howarth, 2007). In pharmacology synergy is discussed in manners of absorption, distribution, metabolism, or elimination (ADME), which are affected by the interactions between drugs and different biological targets in the body, such as enzymes, receptors, or ion channels (Roell et al., 2017). Additionally, synergy may also refer to molecules that target different physiological pathways, but work together towards a common impact (Ulrich-Merzenich, 2009). Finally, another aspect is antagonism where one molecule negatively affects the availability of other nutrients in the organism (Roell et al., 2017).
Traditional nutrition science approaches have employed a reductionist approach by isolating single nutrients to study their effects (Fardet & Rock, 2014), which is oversimplified and does not follow the absolute natural occurrence of multi-nutrient foods (Jacobs et al, 2011) and oversees research findings that have recognised complex nutrient interactions and benefits of combined consumption (Jacobs & Tapsell, 2013). Studying single nutrient actions has been valuable for understanding the major role of nutrients, such as vitamin d to rickets, vitamin C to scurvy and folate to neuraL tube defects (Tulchinsky, 2010), but fail to understand their full potential. The  Food and Nutrition Board publishing the Dietary Reference Intakes (DRIs) has acknowledged the need to further examine nutrient "clusters" and synergistic actions considering these nutrients are not naturally consumed isolated and have crucial metabolic interactions (National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine, 2021).

4.3 Vitamin D, K2 and their Cofactors
Vitamin’s D metabolism is influenced by numerous factors, including vitamin K2, parathormone (PTH), serum calcium (Ca) levels, magnesium (Mg), and zinc (Zn), which are not equally represented in current research (Bleizgys, 2024). Growing evidence supports that, both independently and synergistically, vitamin K2 and vitamin D, have favorable impact on calcium absorption and tightly regulate its metabolism (Singh et al., 2022).
Magnesium is a cofactor of multiple processes required for vitamin D production, activation and action, assisting vitamin D binding to VDP, supporting VDR expression and enabling the activity of hydroxylases (Matias et al., 2023). Magnesium deficiency has been implicated in reduced bone stiffness, increased osteoclast and reduced osteoblast activity, while promoting inflammation and oxidative stress, by interfering with normal PTH and vitamin D activity (Castiglioni et al., 2013).
Zinc (Zn) is also implicated in vitamin D’s functions, regulating the expression of vitamin D-dependent genes by contributing to VDR conformational changes and intensifying the activity of specific promoters (Amos & Razzaque, 2022). On its turn, vitamin D can directly increase the expression of Zn transporters (ZnT10), making it available in increased concentrations (Ahsan et al., 2023).
VDR interacts with both SXR, which is activated by vitamin K2, and RXR, which is dependent on vitamin A (Jones et al, 1998),  providing another proof of the profound interplay between nutrients. SXR-VDR interaction can lead to reduced catabolism of calcitriol and reduced deactivation of 25-hydroxyvitamin D, by suppressing the activity of the VDR-mediated CYP24 promoter, while calcitriol can lead to enhanced reductive recycling of vitamin K2, mutually intensifying each other’s action (Ziemińska et al., 2021).

4.4 Vitamin D, K2 and their common molecular targets 
4.3 Vitamin D, K2 and Osteoblasts
Osteoblastic activity is tightly regulated by Vitamin D, which enhances vitamin K-dependent enzymes’ action (Miyake et al., 2001) and, also, upregulates the expression of genes specific to osteoblasts (Kerner et al., 1989). Osteocalcin’s production, concurrently, depends on vitamin D, while its activation (through carboxylation) depends on vitamin K2, thus providing another apparent explanation of their synergistic potential, especially in bone hydroxyapatite crystal formation (Ferland, 1998; Booth et al., 2004). Before carboxylation, the transcription and translation of the osteocalcin gene are regulated by 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D and affected by its deficiency  (Arbour et al., 1995). Co-administration of both vitamin D and vitamin K2 has been found to increase bone formation through osteocalcin in a time-dependent manner (Poon et al., 2015). 

4.3 Vitamin D, K2 and Matrix Gla protein (MGP)
Matrix Gla protein (MGP) is another common molecular target of both vitamin D and vitamin K2. The gene encoding  MGP contains a vitamin D receptor in its promoter region, and has been shown to increase MGP mRNA expression in humans (Cancela et al., 1990). While calcitriol upregulates MGP expression, its activation is vitamin K-dependent carboxylation.  
4.3 Vitamin D, K2 and Inflammation
Another major area where vitamin D and vitamin K2 overlap is inflammation, as indicated by multiple evidence. Both vitamins have been found to reduce proinflammatory cytokines  (Ziemińska et al., 2021), and more specifically, interleukin-6 (IL-6) and tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-α), which are also implicated in both osteoporotic bone deterioration and calcification of blood vessel wall (Kiel et al., 2001). 
4.5 Vitamin D and K2 Observational Studies 
Dal Canto et al. (2020)  examined the relationship between vitamin D and K levels and subclinical indicators of cardiovascular health, concluding that vitamin D was synergistically associated with vitamin K in terms of cardiac function and all-cause mortality. Their statistical analysis revealed that subjects with low levels of both vitamins had a defect in cardiac structure, which was a considered major threat of all-cause deaths compared to subjects with normal status. 
Van Ballegooijen and coworkers (2017) in an observational prospective study of 231 participants to examine the joint relationship between poor vitamin D and K status and cardiovascular disease, found that  participants with low levels of vitamin D and K had a significant increase in their blood pressure, both systolic and diastolic, as compared with the reference group.
In 2021, Van Ballegooijen and his coworkers, in a larger sample of 970 participants found that 20% had combined low vitamin D and K status, which was further associated with a greater risk  (hazard ratio 1.46) of all-cause mortality after adjusting for potential confounders. 
In another cross-sectional research, Mayer and coworkers (2017)  found that subjects with low levels of vitamin D and vitamin K had an aPWV that was around seven times higher than that of subjects with levels in the highest quartile for both the vitamins. 
Similar findings have been reported for the co-supplementation of vitamin D and vitamin K2 by studies on hypertension and carotid intima-media thickness (Kurnatowska et al., 2015), and in preventing arterial stiffness and vascular calcification (Piscaer et al., 2023)

4.6 Vitamin D and K2 Clinical trials 
Asemi and coworkers (2016) conducted a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled trial co-supplementing vitamin D, K, and calcium and assessing the effect on carotid intima-media thickness (CIMT), glycemic control, lipid concentrations, and biomarkers of inflammation and oxidative stress in type 2 diabetic and overweight patients. After 12 weeks, co-supplementation  improved extremely high left CIMT levels, glycemic parameters, and high-density lipoprotein (HDL) cholesterol levels in patients with diabetes mellitus. No impact on the highest levels of CIMT (right), left and right CIMT (mean levels), other lipid profiles, and oxidative stress indicators were reported. 
Aguayo-Ruiz et al. (2020)  observed that after supplementing several combinations of vitamin D, vitamin K2 and calcium, only the group with vitamin D and vitamin K2 co-supplementation recorded a statistically significant decrease  in HOMA-IR. The double-blind, randomized clinical trial was carried out for three months in 40 patients (30-70 years old) and had three groups: (a) 1,000 IU vitamin D3 + a calcined magnesium placebo (n = 16), (b) 100 μg of vitamin K2 + a calcined magnesium placebo (n = 12), and (c) 1,000 IU vitamin D3 + 100 μg vitamin K2 (n = 12). The supplementation of vitamin D and K2 significantly improved glycemia, HOMA-IR, percentage of pancreatic beta cells, and in carboxylated osteocalcin. 
Similarly, Braam et al. (2004) found that co-supplementation of vitamin D and K provided benefit in postmenopausal women’s vascular function. 
Singh et al. (2022) in their meta-analysis including 12 studies (five randomized controlled trials, six observational studies, and one interventional), involving a total of 8,216 participant, concluded that 11 of the studies showed that vitamin D works synergistically with vitamin K, exhibiting a significant role in improving bone fractures, low bone mineral density, and cardiovascular disorders.

Below is a revised, integrated narrative for Chapter 4, transforming the provided content into a cohesive discussion that emphasizes the synergy between vitamins D and K2, their co-factors, and their relevance to MetS. All studies, references, and key data points are retained, but presented in a more synthesized, story-like manner.

### Chapter 4: The Synergy of Vitamins D and K2 on Metabolic Syndrome and Its Components

#### 4.1 Introduction

As science moves beyond a reductionist lens that isolates single nutrients, increasing attention is paid to how different micronutrients interact to produce results that exceed the sum of their individual effects. Vitamin D and vitamin K2 exemplify this concept of nutrient synergy. While historically viewed primarily through narrow clinical endpoints—vitamin D for rickets prevention, vitamin K for blood clotting—it is now evident that these vitamins have extensive biological roles and complex interplay. Multiple studies have highlighted that co-supplementation of vitamins D and K2 leads to more favorable metabolic and physiological outcomes than either vitamin alone (Singh et al., 2022). Given their overlapping influence on calcium homeostasis, bone integrity, vascular health, and inflammatory pathways, understanding this synergy is especially important in the context of metabolic syndrome (MetS).

#### 4.2 Synergy Definition and Nutritional Approaches

Nutrient synergy occurs when two or more molecules interact in ways that enhance absorption, distribution, metabolism, or elimination (ADME), or target different yet complementary physiological pathways to produce a greater combined effect (Davidson & Howarth, 2007; Roell et al., 2017; Ulrich-Merzenich, 2009). In contrast, antagonistic interactions can diminish nutrient availability. Traditional nutrition research often took a reductionist approach—studying nutrients in isolation—but this method can fail to capture the full complexity of multi-nutrient foods and the metabolic interplay they foster (Fardet & Rock, 2014; Jacobs et al., 2011; Jacobs & Tapsell, 2013). Recognizing these interactions, authoritative bodies now encourage exploring nutrient clusters and synergistic actions (National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine, 2021).

#### 4.3 Vitamin D, K2, and Their Cofactors

Vitamin D’s metabolism and function depend on various other nutrients and hormones, including vitamin K2, parathyroid hormone (PTH), calcium (Ca), magnesium (Mg), and zinc (Zn) (Bleizgys, 2024). Both vitamin D and K2 have been shown to jointly improve calcium absorption and more tightly regulate its metabolism, impacting bone health, vascular integrity, and potentially key aspects of MetS (Singh et al., 2022).

Magnesium, a cofactor essential for vitamin D production, activation, and receptor (VDR) function, modulates vitamin D’s efficacy. Deficiencies in Mg can alter osteoblast and osteoclast activity, inflammation, and oxidative stress, ultimately interfering with both vitamin D and PTH actions (Matias et al., 2023; Castiglioni et al., 2013). Zinc similarly supports vitamin D function by influencing VDR conformation and promoter activity (Amos & Razzaque, 2022), and vitamin D can, in turn, increase zinc transporter expression (Ahsan et al., 2023).

Moreover, VDR interacts with steroid and xenobiotic receptor (SXR)—activated by vitamin K2—and retinoid X receptor (RXR)—dependent on vitamin A—further evidencing the intricate nutrient network. SXR-VDR interactions can reduce calcitriol catabolism and slow deactivation of 25-hydroxyvitamin D, while calcitriol enhances the reductive recycling of vitamin K2 (Ziemińska et al., 2021). Such mutually reinforcing mechanisms underscore how vitamin D and K2 do not simply co-exist but actively potentiate each other’s metabolic roles.

#### 4.4 Vitamin D, K2, and Their Common Molecular Targets

**Vitamin D, K2, and Osteoblasts:**  
Bone-forming osteoblasts rely heavily on both vitamin D and K2. Vitamin D stimulates osteoblast gene expression, while vitamin K2 is critical for γ-carboxylation of osteocalcin, enabling this protein to bind calcium effectively. Without vitamin K2, vitamin D-driven osteocalcin production cannot achieve full functionality, and bone mineralization may be suboptimal (Miyake et al., 2001; Kerner et al., 1989; Ferland, 1998; Booth et al., 2004). Research shows that co-administration of vitamin D and K2 can time-dependently increase bone formation through osteocalcin modification (Poon et al., 2015), illustrating a clear synergy relevant for MetS-related skeletal issues.

**Vitamin D, K2, and Matrix Gla Protein (MGP):**  
MGP is another shared target. Vitamin D upregulates MGP gene expression (Cancela et al., 1990), an action essential for inhibiting ectopic calcification in blood vessels. However, MGP requires vitamin K-dependent carboxylation to function optimally. Thus, vitamin D ensures MGP is produced, but vitamin K2 ensures it is activated, together offering a protective mechanism against vascular calcification and the cardiovascular complications often seen in MetS.

**Vitamin D, K2, and Inflammation:**  
Chronic low-grade inflammation is integral to MetS. Both vitamin D and K2 have been found to reduce pro-inflammatory cytokines, such as IL-6 and TNF-α, which are involved not only in inflammatory states but also in bone deterioration and vascular calcification (Ziemińska et al., 2021; Kiel et al., 2001). By jointly modulating inflammation, D and K2 may better address the inflammatory dimension of MetS than either vitamin alone.

#### 4.5 Vitamin D and K2 Observational Studies

Observational research supports the idea that combined low levels of vitamin D and K have more detrimental effects than low levels of either nutrient alone. Dal Canto et al. (2020) associated low vitamin D and K status with cardiac structural defects and an increased threat of all-cause mortality. Similarly, Van Ballegooijen et al. (2017) found that individuals jointly deficient in vitamins D and K presented with significantly elevated systolic and diastolic blood pressure, underscoring a synergistic detriment to cardiovascular health. Expanding their sample, Van Ballegooijen and coworkers (2021), in a study of 970 participants, noted that about 20% had combined low D and K status, correlating with a hazard ratio of 1.46 for all-cause mortality. Mayer et al. (2017) further demonstrated that subjects with simultaneously low vitamin D and K levels had arterial pulse wave velocity (aPWV) around seven times higher than those in the highest quartile for both vitamins, indicating substantially greater vascular stiffness. Comparable findings extend to improvements in hypertension and carotid intima-media thickness (Kurnatowska et al., 2015), as well as prevention of arterial stiffness and vascular calcification (Piscaer et al., 2023). These observational results collectively underscore a metabolic synergy between vitamins D and K2 that translates into tangible clinical outcomes relevant to MetS.

#### 4.6 Vitamin D and K2 Clinical Trials

Interventional studies reinforce these observational findings. Asemi et al. (2016), in a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled trial supplementing vitamin D, K, and calcium in type 2 diabetic, overweight patients, demonstrated improvements in glycemic parameters and HDL cholesterol levels, alongside reduced left carotid intima-media thickness (CIMT). Although not all CIMT measures changed, these targeted metabolic and vascular improvements highlight the synergy in specific MetS-related endpoints.

Aguayo-Ruiz et al. (2020) provided further confirmation. Their three-month double-blind, randomized trial, dividing 40 patients into groups receiving vitamin D, vitamin K2, or both, found that only the combination group achieved statistically significant reductions in HOMA-IR, glycemia, and improved β-cell function and carboxylated osteocalcin. Such data strongly suggest that vitamin D and K2 co-supplementation produces metabolic benefits that cannot be attributed to either nutrient independently.

Another example comes from Braam et al. (2004), who observed that combining vitamin D and K improved vascular function in postmenopausal women. Meanwhile, Singh et al. (2022) synthesized data from 12 studies (five RCTs, six observational studies, and one interventional study) involving 8,216 participants. They concluded that in 11 of these studies, vitamin D worked synergistically with K to significantly improve bone outcomes (e.g., fractures and low bone mineral density) and reduce cardiovascular disorders, conditions that often overlap with MetS-associated risks.

---

### Conclusion

The emerging story from research on vitamin D and K2 synergy is one of complementary actions converging on critical metabolic pathways that underlie MetS. Both vitamins independently influence calcium homeostasis, bone metabolism, vascular integrity, and inflammation, but together they appear to offer enhanced protection and therapeutic potential. Observational and clinical trial data consistently indicate that combining vitamin D and K2 yields better glycemic control, improved lipid profiles, reduced arterial stiffness, and favorable changes in bone and cardiovascular parameters compared to single nutrient interventions.

Given the complexity of MetS—spanning glucose dysregulation, vascular health, adiposity, and inflammation—the multifaceted synergy of vitamin D and K2 may represent a promising avenue for more effective and holistic interventions. As research progresses, clearer dosing guidelines, patient stratification, and the integration of co-factors such as magnesium and zinc may further refine strategies that leverage this synergy, ultimately improving outcomes for individuals at risk of or living with MetS.
